RATING: 8.5
Thematic, strategic tile-layer with a strong market component.

There are several options each turn, many of which can be performed multiple times, and there are a myriad of things to consider and consequences for the actions.  Many actions also cannot be performed unless you have the coveted consumed polyp tiles (which you win by eating/attacking other coral reefs).  The game essentially requires you to fight against other corals, in order to advance yourself, and you also want to set up your own coral so that your parrotfish can consume it, and thus score points.  Of course, you will need to take turns to manipulate the coral hierarchy (market) in order to: a)make sure your corals are stronger than others, so they can attack and not be attacked, and b)to ensure that your corals score more points after they are consumed by your parrotfish.

The components are great, the rule book leaves a bit to be desired (but it's not as bad as it's made out to be), the theme is great and apparently very realistic, and the gameplay is very deep and strategic.  Seems to scale well for 2-4 players, and has a few different paths to victory.

Own the [boardgame=22304]Reef Encounters of the Second Kind[/boardgame] expansion.

RATING: 8.5
Reef Encounter is hard to learn by yourself, because to understand why you'd want to take a given action, you need to understand the game as a whole.  So this one is tough to learn and teach, but after a few turns it should click.  Your first game should definitely be approached as a "learning game".  

Fortunately, locking in color dominance doesn't come until the latter half of the game and most players should have a grasp of what's going on before that time comes.  Speaking of dominance, there's this really cool mechanic for flipping a bunch of tiles rather than just the one you want, so each decision definitely has knock-on effects.  For the first few games you play, delving into that aspect of the game will be "too deep"; just don't worry about it.

There's room for interesting tactical plays here, especially in terms of cutting big coral clusters up and protecting yourself from attack.  It's very much war on the table, and you're constantly thinking about how to attack and defend.  It's a little like Tigris & Euphrates in how it feels, but it plays out much differently.

It's a fun and very engaging game.  A brain burner, but not super bad.  The rules aren't very complex, but they're hard to grasp.  It has the honor of being "surprising woman friendly".

RATING: 7
'The prawn game' has rather unfairly become the byword in my group for rule impenetrability.  It's actually not too tough a game, in terms of rule complexity, but there's no thematic redundancy to help you out - if you're hazy on a rule, there's no way you can lean on your background understanding of the way coral reefs work, say, to kickstart a reminder what to do.  Instead, you have to go and read the rule again.  As a result, the arbitrariness of the theme is continually flaunted in your face.  'The prawns are laughing at me', I thought, as I effed and blinded and reloaded the seventeen phases into my short term memory ('phase 0: load the seventeen phases into etc etc').

RATING: 10
2-4(3)

UPDATE: A lot of quirky recordkeeping that can be easily forgotten: keeping track of which tiles are in front of or behind the screen, only placing four tiles from behind the screen, only laying corals twice on a turn, etc. but it doesn't take all that long to get the hang of it.

Aside from the quirks and the rulebook, it's fantastic. Some push-your-luck in balancing getting the cube you want with getting that cube when it's full of tiles. More press-your-luck in deciding when to consume (though getting that first consume seems pretty crucial). Plays really well with two. It's "stabby", but "thinky stabby" (as in, a conflict-ridden optimization problem) rather than "stabby stabby" (as in, the objective is simply to attack others, ala Small World and its ilk) ... to the point where my wife gets angry at me when we play (love it!).

After playing, I understand the "stock manipulation" categorization (though I don't enjoy deconstructing games so surgically) ala Acquire. I think this one is great. Up to a 10.

-------------------------------------------------------

I really, really liked my play of this. There is a lot going on and the rules are a mess and the gameplay is pretty complicated. Still. There are a lot of fascinating elements. Going for corals quickly (it's not too hard to pull out some scorable corals early on) or try to drop a huge coral late. The coral strength and consuming corals is fascinating, not least because you want to consume opponents' corals but also because you often will also want to consume your own coral. That you can't consume all coral types (well, you can, if you include the initial token) means that you're certainly going to be gaming against certain colors. Scoring only at the beginning of a round has sort of a press-your-luck element ... it's all really excellent and I'm eager to explore further.

RATING: 7
Strip away the unusual aquatic theme and Reef Encounter can be viewed as a game of set collection with an element of stock market manipulation.  Yet nothing in this game is quite that straightforward.  The set collection has a spacial element - our sets are laid on a grid for others to nibble away at before we can collect them.  As well as determining value, that stock market also determines the relative strengths of the varieties of coral we are collecting, which are highly significant when it comes to nibbling.  That sort of inter-dependence runs through every element of Reef Encounter, which is one of the reasons it is hard to get your head around it at first.  The other is a rulebook that really shouldn't have left the object of the game until last.  Get over those bumps and there is a challenging, thinky, interactive game that still seems very novel. 

The artwork of the R&D edition is a little washed out for my taste.  While the patterns of the white and grey polyps are quite different, they are a little too close in colour.  Production quality is very good and those shrimps are very cute.

RATING: 8
So far I've rated this game an 8 because there's a lot to like about it.  The things that keep it from being higher: a) There's a lot of downtime especially with over-analytical players because there are so many choices and your decisions are all intertwined with each other, b) this game really rewards spatial reasoning, which means that I'm really not that good at it, c) the colors are confusing.  You have player colors (yellow, red, blue, green), coral polyp tile colors and associated larva cubes (yellow, pink, orange, white, grey), and algae cylinder colors (red, green, blue, purple).  None of which relate to each other.  In addition, the pink cubes don't seem to match the pink tiles very well, and the purple cylinders don't match the purple symbol on the coral strength tiles very well, d) the game is slightly more strategic (as opposed to tactical) than I'd like - I prefer a higher balance of tactics to strategy, whereas I would say tactics and strategy are about equal in Reef Encounter...  possibly slightly tilted toward strategy, e) the box insert sucks.

All in all, though, a good, deep game with lots of interaction, and lots of interesting decisions.

RATING: 8
A competitive tactical exercise in growing and destroying coral reefs.  The game is very good and the theme is well-integrated, but I am not sure that it makes any sense.

Why are some corals worth more than other corals?  It seems like this started as a variant to Acquire: the corals abstractly represent business entities fighting for market share.  The board that displays which corals dominate other corals then makes a lot more sense: this represents some kind of aggregation of stock price, consumer sentiment, and legal conflict.

RATING: 8
The rule book wording is terrible and anyone trying to learn the game from them is bound to find the game awkward at best.  If you are fortunate enough to be taught the game by someone who knows the rules, then the game is a solid design with a nice theme.  At its heart the game is a majorities game, but the factors that influence the scores is where the beauty lies.  Manipulating the coral hierarchy is key and knowing when to lock in a coral will ensure a players success.  Despite the board and the shrimples, the game is really played out on the influence board.  Definitely worth a playing, but please, please learn it from someone and skip the rulebook. [GTI: APSD]

RATING: 8
[i]Posted: 4/16/06, Rated: 8[/i]

A gorgeous game with lots of [i]bits[/i] -- a common trait among Richard Breese games (along with player screens).  The shrimps alone are almost legendary.

The theme is interesting and educational.  The components look great, in my opinion.  The [i]rules...[/i] frankly the rules [i]stink[/i].  This is not to say that it is a bad game.  Quite the contrary: I really enjoy [i]Reef Encounter[/i].  But if you don't have the benefit of learning the game from someone who has played it before, then trying to learn from scratch really hurts.  Minus one point for the rules.

[i]Reef Encounter[/i] introduced me to the unique mechanism of sharing a number of common boards.  It is a tile laying game, that plays out like a series of small area control games.  You use your shrimps to defend your territory.

If that was all there was to the game, then it would be good.  A little average, but still good.  The variable color-dominance pushes [i]Reef Encounter[/i] towards being a great game.  Just like rock-paper-scissors, certain color tiles can "eat" (overbuild) some colors, while being weak to others.  However, as the game progresses, you have the ability to change the relationships.  You can make a particular color very strong, able to "eat" more colors than it could at the start of the game.  This mechanic also influences the scoring at the end of the game, and so it is doubly-important.

The tactical challenges of a tile-laying game combined with an overarching strategic element make for an exciting game.  Add an interesting, eye-catching theme, and [i]Reef Encounter[/i] is a hit.

RATING: 6
SetCollection, Solo

Game flow: Like the Gulf Stream.
Strategic depth: Like the ocean.
Theme: Dripping with it.
  
I really like this game!  Of Richard Breese's offerings, this one hits the nail on the head from the bat.  The game is simpler in some ways than T&E (coral interactions between each other and the shrimp, the meat of the game), and more complex in others (more options that can be bought using consumed coral tiles, and the coral strength/algae relationships).
  
I have to admit that I like the theme of Reef Encounter better than T&E, but it's nice to have both games on hand for variety.  Both are excellent, classic games.  Incidentally, both share certain characteristics with Sackson's Acquire, another classic game... but that's another story.
  
I keep this game around (even though it would make pretty awesome trade fodder) for the theme and the tile-laying T&E/Acquire-ish mechanics.  I really like the strategies here, particularly the limited shrimps, and the coral chomping.  The algae interactions are nicely done, and give the game a dynamic aspect.  This can induce analysis paralysis, of course... but I like the game regardless because of that.  You really do need to plan ahead and determine exactly what algae tiles your opponents will want to lock down, and when they can do it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 9
This game is a brain burner. It needs real skill to work the right balance between growing corals, keeping them high in the dominance table to score more points and be able with another colour to feed on your main one so to provide you with necessary surplus tiles (necessary to fast grow bigger corals and to supply you with larvas if needed). Of course this have to be done keeping an eye on your opponent and eating if necessary and possible their polips as well.
Last but not least, the resupply system hamper players going for just one colour. Often you get just larva or polyp tiles needed, not both, and it's up to you choose what to get.
Multicolour tactic works better, but make more challenging to keep control on the dominance table.
A very good and original game, with some system taken by T&E.
If you like the latter, it's a must.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8.5


[b]7/18/2013[/b]
Reef Encounter shares some similarities to two games that I really admire- Tigris and Euphrates and Acquire. Here you have personal groups of tiles rather than the "shared" groups of T&E. The comparison to Acquire is faint- the game has tiles which are played in a sort of abstract fashion- but in RE there is a market in which you adjust the value of the different chains (acquire) or colors (RE) as opposed to the system present in Acquire. Instead of shares in the various colors/chains you create your own groups and manipulate the market to increase the values of your favorite colors. (the tiles you intend to score at game end) 

The game is very interactive and allows for some creative tactical play. The game does not hold your hand, against skilled opponents you will be punished for misplays and overly aggressive tile plays. It's a rather tight game when play correctly- driven by an almost "race-like" game progression, where the tempo and rhythm of the game is very palatable. 

The game plays well through all player counts, though I suspect 3P is it's sweet spot with a relatively close 2P game as well. Four players see's the game get more tactical and chaotic, whereas with two, the game feels it's most strategic and abstract. 

It's rules are more opaque than the game plays out. It might take a couple of plays to feel like you know what you're doing. It's a pretty heavy game. I'd say heavier that T&E, not by much. It feels similar in depth, though the rules are more lush given the market and rules for eaten tiles and such. 

I'm very glad "nyuhase" has this game. If she didn't have it, this would be one of the first games I'd buy. It's that good. I imagine it will continue to be in my top tier of games for some time.

RATING: 6
Cube confusion!

No but seriously, here are some strategies I'm working on.

Play to the initial tile dropped into your parrotfish. Try to pick one that is what some but not all of the other players are going for. Do not got behind in the shrimp eating. Stay with the leader or be the leader. Remember that the final coral hierarchy determines the score, not just the locked ones. Try to place last and keep some consumed polyp tiles for last score adjustment.

This game by Richard breese reminds you so very much of Key Harvest which is also the same in at least 3-4 ways.

1) Competing for won tiles.
2) Various actions allow you to gain privelages that when timed properly exploit ways to increase basic scoring into multiplied scoring.
3) The victory points spreads end up being very close.
4) The game seems extremely delicate... in that if you dont take advantage of various scoring opportunities... you will not be blown away,  but you certainly won't win either.

Nice game, but a bit shy on excitment.

RATING: 6
UPDATE: I played this again recently and it forced me to downgrade my rating. I think Reef Encounter is really clever for about the first half of the game, but once players have consumed a couple reefs and a few corals have been locked, the game totally dries up as competition for space completely falls off and the room for maneuver narrows. I wish the game could remain more consistently interesting for the entire length. I'm optimistic that the expansion will ultimately provide in this area, but my enthusiasm for the base game just isn't what it used to be.

I played the Z-Man games edition for the first time, and they've done a pretty nice job with it. The day-glo cubes are a little jarring, but other than that the darker, highly saturated colors have a nice look. I personally prefer the brighter colors of the original, even with the slightly too-close white and grey tiles, but the new one is nice too.
---
Capsule Review: http://homepage.mac.com/c_farrell/iblog/C2097221587/E648403382/index.html

I like Reef Encounter, but time has shown it to not have as much replayability as I'd like. I like the wonderful colors, I like the thematic gameplay, and I think it does present the player with lots of interesting tactical decision points ... but the tile-laying portion is so reminiscent of Tigris & Euphrates, and T&E is just a vastly superior game by any measure. The growth of the reefs is just too constrained, and the on-board play doesn't have the dynamicism or the organic feel of Tigris & Euphrates. Reefs expand from a couple tiles to 5 or 6, and then get eaten, and once a few have been eaten, the board becomes too sparse, with not enough interaction.

Still, having said all this, Reef Encounter is still a good game which I'm happy to own and happier to play than most people I game with. But like most every Richard Breese game, it seems to have greatness in its grasp but not quite be able to achieve it.

RATING: 4
Updated 7/12/10.

[thing=12962]Reef Encounter[/thing] was a game that I’d long desired, but that I had refrained from purchasing because I had feared that it wouldn’t resonate with any of my traditional gaming partners.  Billed as some kind of underwater bastard child of [thing=42]Tigris & Euphrates[/thing] and [thing=5]Acquire[/thing] (both of which I love), I had hoped the game to be superficially innocuous enough to engage my not-yet-hardcore gaming group, but laden with enough deep and subtle strategies to scratch my itch.  

While I’m certain that such strategies exist, they were entirely beyond my grasp in the first two plays.  In both games, my wife won handily by simply feeding her parrotfish regularly, while positioning her coral in a way that stifled any hope I had for growth.  In fact, I was unable to consume a single tile in the first game.  It’s not the losing that I mind, mind you, but that I’m unable to even conceive of a counter-measure to her incredibly simple strategy (Build a 5-6 tile coral; place a shrimp.  Eat said coral; build another 5-6 tile coral).  I can’t help but feel that I’m either playing by incorrect rules or that I’m simply inconsolably (and perhaps irreparably) out-of-sync with the game.  Meanwhile, my failing as an opponent has also led my wife to dismiss the game as simplistic and childish (the cutesy eyes on the shrimp didn’t help). :soblue:

One of the primary sources of my difficulty was my inability to maintain a steady flow of tiles and cubes of the right colors.  In the first game, by the end of the first turn, I had only two tiles behind my screen.  Even after passing for the next two turns, I was unable to do anything more than grow a two-tile coral, which seemed to be a waste of an action.  It reminded me a bit of [thing=43015]Hansa Teutonica[/thing]’s early game, where players can accomplish so little on such an open board, that the game seems a bit pointless.  But where HT quickly ramps up into a mad scramble in the wake of an imminent end, Reef Encounter simply putters along at this pace until the game ends (which was at least mercifully quickly). 

In other games (e.g., [thing=28720]Brass[/thing], [thing=12681]Neuland[/thing]), such a frustrating start has motivated me to grapple with the strategy, ponder the game in my free time, and put it back on the table as often as possible until I truly understood it.  Perhaps because at this stage I’ve yet to even glimpse the game’s tantalizing potential, or perhaps because many aspects of the game don’t appeal to me in practice (the tile draw on the open sea board strikes me as both too random, and too upkeep-heavy to justify the mechanic; flipping the dominance tiles in colored sets is AP-inducing, but not in a fun “thinky” way), Reef Encounter has not had that effect.  Instead, I’m inclined to simply write it off as a game with which I just don’t connect.  

One final note: I was a bit disappointed with the components of the Z-man edition.  The tiles were smaller than I thought; the paint on the algae cylinders isn’t remotely close to the color on the dominance tiles; the slots to assemble the parrotfish tiles were too narrow, resulting in considerable damage to the cardboard components when assembled (we’ve just left them assembled and out of the box for fear of doing more damage when taking them apart); the included bag was too small to hold all the tiles and allow for shuffling; and the linen texture on the box and tiles feels unpleasantly artificial.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Richard Breese's magnum opus. This is a tile laying game similar to Tigris & Euphrates. Surprisingly, I suspect I might prefer this to T&E but I will need to play both titles more before I decide. Reef Encounter (like T&E) plays like Go or Chess, offering a broad strategic horizon, emphasis on maneuver, and more direct conflict among players. This is in contrast to most other euro "strategy" games that seem to emphasize efficiency and resource management. The positional aspect of Reef Encounter in particular feels like a war game stripped of its theme and reduced to its bare essence. I've played with 2, 3, and 4 players and although it's an enjoyable game with any number of players, the fewer the better. It's especially good with 2 and 3, becoming slightly chaotic with 4.

The game itself is beautiful but I have mixed feelings about the theme. It's rather dry, despite modeling an underwater environment (ahaw, how droll I am) but the theme is definitely not pasted on. Playing Reef Encounter really does evoke the feeling of an underwater ecosystem, to a certain degree at least (it's nothing like, say, American Megafauna. This is still a euro afterall).

UPDATE: After several plays of this and T&E, I think T&E is a significantly better game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
The game here is all about timing your eating. The longer you wait, the better idea you'll have regarding the valuation of the various corals and the more coral you'll get per bite, but you could find yourself not eating all four of your shrimp as the game ends before you really get going. Go quickly and you can control the tempo and when the game ends, but your coral might not be the most valuable ones.

Turn by turn, while some are of the take some tiles and pass variety, there are opportunities for explosive play where one does tricks with how the various mechanisms interrelate.

The game can be vicious as well, because attacking someone else's coral reef not only takes them down a peg, it also boosts your own capacities to control the game.

The game itself is an interesting simulation, as while you do not take on the role of any particular member of the ecosystem, by using the rules to try and win, you do recreate it. Documentary style.

The game's biggest flaw is its barrier to entry. The rules. There are quite a few things you need to learn before you can start playing. It flows well once you start, but getting there can be rough, and the first game will be a learning game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Based on 1 play with 2 players:

The six is based off of how much fun I eventually had with the game by the end of our first game, though I think my real rating could go in either direction.

First, the game's rulebook really makes your first play a truly unpleasant experience. It opts for its own "flavorful" terminology over standard terms at every turn, so it takes you a good half of the game before you even understand what you're doing.

Second, the strategy in your first game is going to seem very opaque indeed. There's a LOT going on here, and the complex machinery of interwoven systems is really something to behold. It's interesting, and possibly deep, but...

Third, at times the game can feel too restrictive. Once you set down a path you're pretty stuck in it, and I often found myself feeling handcuffed by my options.

Fourth, the downtime is a nightmare. I can't imagine playing this with four.

If complex, brain-burning interplay of systems is your thing this is probably a must-play. But for me, I think I'd rather have something that is a straight-up puzzle or something a little more free-form.

Update: This has been sitting on my shelf for years, unplayed. The idea of ever going back to that rulebook has lost all appeal. Will probably trade.

RATING: 8.5
While I am not generally enamoured of tile placement games, this one is exceptional.  The intricate blend of game mechanics is marvelous - drafting, set collection, tile placement, area control, and commodity speculation are all here.  Impressively (and contrary to the opinion of many), the gameplay does fit the theme well; corals grow, become eroded by their neighbours, are eaten by parrotfishes, and vie for dominance during the game, and doing these things feels "natural".  The mechanism by which consumed polyps can be exchanged for algae that one uses to alter the hierarchy of different coral types is brilliant.  The ideas are executed masterfully, even if the shit rulebook might make one think otherwise (at least until you play your first game).  Highly recommended!

RATING: 8
Wow! I cannot compare the mechanics to any other eurogame, and along with some very tough decisions during the game, this has resulted in a high rating. The idea of the corals' change in strength by flipping the tiles back and forth throughout the game is great, and even though you might have eaten a lot of coral tiles during the game, you won't win if your coral tiles are weak. Also, the other players might suddenly make a strong play of certain polyp tiles which you have locked earlier, which makes it very hard to ruin their plans!

It's also a delightfully aggressive euro game, which is a scarcity in my opinion, and this makes it stand even more out from the rest of the euro games. It plays very well with 2 and 3; 4 isn't great as the boards change too much during your turns so it's hard to plan ahead and there isn't anything to do when it's not your time.

As if that wasn't enough, the game has nice artwork and a unique theme, and though I don't really care for those two things in a board game, it obviously never detracts from a rating when the artwork is nice to look at. Great game!

Rated 7 after one play, but this is a tough one to rate. This might be one of the more convoluted games I've played. The rules are not that complicated, but good play is not immediately clear. I enjoy watching the different corals played on the board, but how to best use tactics to score is hard to "grok" on a first play.

Update: Well, I eventually sold this one in an auction. This one sat on the shelf for quite some time. Too hard for my wife, and too many of my local gaming friends were uninterested. I think it's a good game, but I got tired watching it collect dust.

RATING: 7
I don't usually make comments about box art or rulebooks, but I feel that the Z-Man games release has some major problems with these.  First of all, the product description on the back of the box looks like it was typeset by an 8-year-old; if I hadn't read about this game in advance, there's a good chance I would've decided to never buy it simply based on the font on the back of the box.  The rules are written with absolutely horrible grammar.  Naturally, I don't expect perfectly written rules from board games, especially since so many are translated from German or other languages, but this one was the worst I've seen in quite a while.

I've heard many complaints that the mangled rules were difficult to understand, but I didn't find this the case...I just winced even 15 seconds while reading them.

That pickiness aside, I liked the game ("interesting"), but I wouldn't say I loved it or hated it.  I thought I'd be more excited about the gameplay from reading the rules, but the game is simply "okay" to me.  It seems to me that with smaller numbers of players, one player with the right set of tiles can blitz through to the end (quickly eating all shrimp) and edge out players who are agonizing over perfect reefs and coral card heirarchies.

RATING: 9
There are loads of things to consider with each move.  I like the dual nature of the tiles and determining the best timing as to when it is best to use them as cash on hand for future positioning, or when to use them as commodities.  
There is also a strong commodity speculation aspect in the early game that I find fascinating, and then the fight for coral dominance for final scoring.  These aspects, along with the consumption of corals by other corals create a very interesting tug of war dynamic as your position tends to ebb and flow throughout the game.

The rule book is a pain... watch some videos instead.  This is one of those games where everything moves in cycles, and almost like intertwined gears, so there is no easy entry point to start explaining the game.

RATING: 8
There is a great level of planning and caginess in this game. The theme is quirky and it works well. I feel like there is so much going on that I can't get my head around it. At least in that sense its reminiscent of Tigris and Euphrates. Beyond that, I'm not sure the comparisons are so apt.

One thing I'm not so sure of is the start position balance. You get more tiles if you go later in the turn order...and there are equal boards to the number of players so, theoretically, everyone should get to start with the benefit of a "bonus" tile if they choose. But I'd like to see some stats for thousands of plays with win percentage from each position. Just curious. 

Either way, this game has beautiful components (I've only played the reprint version, but have seen the original, too). 

The variable game ending is where the game derives its tension, more than anything. I think its THAT mechanic which is most similar to Tigris and Euphrates. I also really like the ability to modify your (or your opponent's) scores after the points are 'in the bank' or parrotfish as it were.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
Reef Encounter is a heavy tile laying and stock manipulation game by indie game designer Richard Breese. The core concept behind the game is controlling large polyp clusters on an undersea rock (guarded by your shrimp) and then having them devoured by your feeder fish. The game play is essentially a multi-nuanced tile laying system where one collects both tiles and cubes. The cubes must be used to allow you to place the tiles, and the value of the tiles is charted on another board. Said value is important for both the end game scoring and in game play, as the value of the tile is tied directly to its ability in game to be placed over the top of other tiles, thus generating another resource, eaten tiles, which are very important as they can be used to manipulate the tile dominance structure. Thus, the resource structure basically breaks down into: tiles, cubes used to play tiles, tiles which you have consumed with other tiles. The previously consumed tiles are of particular importance because they can be used not only to change or lock tile dominance states on a sub-board, but they can also be exchanged for cubes (thus allowing the placement of yet more tiles) or be reused onto the board with a normal cube play. Yes, that was a summary. Yes, this game has a lot going on.

Learning when to just take your polyp tiles and larva cube and end your turn rather than play stuff out is an important skill. My initial impulse was to want to do something semi-major each turn, and Reef Encounter instead encourages building up some resources slowly, and then letting them explode in a mad rush of activity. Seperate boards, while a novel new idea, seem to make the game more defensive than I might like. I don't mind defensive postures being possible, but in Reef shutting off entire sections of a given board is not only possible, but recommended, which limits interactions between the players a bit more than I would like. This isn't to say that you aren't playing against them directly at all, everything is very dependent on what the opposition is doing, I just think the overall defensive nature of this game didn't mesh with my particular temperment for whatever reason. There are many interesting tricks you can pull once you start figuring out the mechanics, but I kept feeling as though the game was missing something. RE is not bad by any means, just not top tier.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
It is unfortunate that with 4 players the feel of the game is so different (and in my opinion, less) than it is with 2. With 2, the game is cerebral and very strategic&mdash;rather like a chess match, but in a completely different way. With 4 there is distinct downtime, lack of player interaction (resistance against your plans is much more diffuse), and probably worst of all too much change on the boards between player turns. There are precisely two moments when you need to pay attention to what others do: when they eat a coral, and when they take new tiles. The rest is simply not important (unless you desire to pick up new tricks).

Despite the somewhat questionable scalability, the game structure is so unique and so completely non-linear (read: circular) that you are likely to find yourself doing things which are seemingly the complete opposite of your Grand Master Plan. You can interpret this as a weakness, because you not only have your opponent to deal with, but also the admittedly hard game mechanics which, at times, seem to delight in making things really hard for you. This is an issue you should not underestimate: putting this game on the table 'just to see what it is like' is a clear mistake. Players will wonder what happened to their brains, and noone will have a proper idea how to go about making things happen in a way they envisage. But playing more often, gaining experience as you go, is definitely rewarded. It becomes less of a fight against the mechanics and more of 'going with the flow'. As beginner you simply don't fully comprehend their scope yet. Every time you play you learn a little more, meaning that this is a title you won't soon tire of. Even when I need to consider the rather painful fact that my most common opponent is my girlfriend who is an absolutely ruthless player who beats the living daylights out of me, and delights in making things really, REALLY hard for me.

This game is not for wussies: you need to [i]think[/i] here. But if that is not a problem, go for it. Clear rules, beautiful graphics in whatever version, and a depth and uniqueness in gameplay to put any regular boardgame to shame. It is sometimes compared to Euphrat & Tigris, but the comparison only goes so far. Knizias title is more incremental, whereas Reef Encounter is ... well, circular. Or perhaps wavy, if that makes any sense. Reef Encounter is by far the best of Richard Breese's designs to date as well one of the best boardgames I have ever played&mdash;my sincere compliments to him.

RATING: 6.5
What looks like a pretty little game is actually a multi-layered abstract strategy challenge.  Players are trying to manipulate growing coral to maximize the opportunity to eat it, while balancing the imposition of other players and keeping an eye one which type of coral dominates in that area.

On the plus side, I like the idea, the components and the depth.  But I felt like there is a lot of struggling for very small net gains, and downtime between turns is a real issue.

For a light-confrontation game, I think I'd prefer Tigris & Euphrates - but still would play this one again if offered in the future.

RATING: 2
There are some neat ideas in this, and there is about 20 minutes of fun in the multi-hour slog.  But the basic structure of the game sets it apart as being less fun than oral surgery.  Most games (wait, lemme check... okay, every game I have) progress towards an ending.  I don't mean just being linear, but there is an end condition that the game is going towards.  This game is structured to head towards a reset.  Over and over.  Every time a coral gets eaten, the board is opened up again.  Imagine playing Go, but whenever you block out a section of territory, you sweep off all the involved stones, score some points, and keep going.  That's what this is like.  It is like playing 12 sequential (admittedly clever) games nonstop.  This could be a great game if that base structure was changed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
Interesting game that kind of reminds me of cross between Tigris&Euphrates and Mykerinos.  You are building up coral reefs, trying to balance decisions on whether to expand them further (for more potential points), or "eat them" for immediate points.  There are some good tactics to it as your "shrimp" are used to defend your coral from invading coral.  I like the mechanics quite a bit.  On the negative, there are a lot of different terms (for components) that you have to get your head around before a game explanation makes sense.  Luckily, the theme works well so that helps.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
Players nuture their own coral on a reef in the hope they get to eat it!  A tile placement/conflict game that is very reminiscent of Euphrat & Tigris.  Players seed the reef boards with a coral cube, allowing polyp tiles to be spread out over the reef, gobbling up neighbouring coral, and protected by shrimp.  If your coral becomes large enough, it can be eaten by your fish, with excess tiles being tossed into your "fish" cup for points at the end of the game.  Additional rules for flip-flopping the dominance of coral with respect to each other, which figures strongly in the final points tally (stronger coral worth more).  Lots to think about, and a pretty good game.  Seems to lack the elegance of Euphrat & Tigris though.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 5
This is an interesting tile laying game.  The shrimples are awesome!  Strategy is tough because you have to be tactical on the board and manage your resources which are: tiles in front of your screen, tiles behind your screen, and cubes.  Most of these resources have dual functions as well.  On the board you will be trying to make clumps of similar polyp tiles and protect them with your shrimp.  The polyp tiles have a hierarchy of which colored polyp tiles are dominant over another type.  Things get tricky as you can change the hierarchy and also lock aspects of the hierarchy in place which helps with end game scoring.  It's a very thoughtful but easy for players to lose themselves in analysis paralysis, so it might be better to play with a low player count to reduce downtime.  I think the biggest caveat is that there is a luck of the draw element with starting tiles/what tiles end up with what cubes and a lot of chaos regarding which tiles are taken with cubes.  In the end it's interesting but dry... kinda funny given the theme.

RATING: 7.5
The gameplay is very interesting and also very smooth.  The mechanics all interlock very well giving a lot to think about but no impression of clunkiness.  It's really well designed, and seems very deep (more plays will tell for sure).

As an added bonus, the components are beautiful and the theme is very enjoyable (and the mechanics fit the theme very well.  

Note however that I would not want to play this with more than 2 players, both because of the downtime and the loss of control.  But with 2 players this is a brilliant game.  My only fear is that it will be too long for us to get it onto the table very much.

One other minor complaint is the randomness in which tiles are available; I generally prefer no-luck games.  But because there are five options to draft from, this does not feel too restrictive - much better than the random tile draw in Tigris&Euphrates, which I find very annoying.  Here the randomness elements are minor enough to not bother me too much.  

1/8/2013 - With a dozen 2-player plays under our belt, I've upgraded this to a "9".  It seems like every game we discover a new strategy.  And the gameplay is so smooth and satisfying - I love it!

12/24/2017
After a number more plays this has lost of a little bit of it's shine. Now it feels more like just a set-collection game, which of course at its heart it is, and the randomness bothers me a bit more.  Still a good game though.

RATING: 6
A game that lives up to its reputation of being a very clever game which lends itself to AP. I'll probably never be very good at a spatial game like this, but I actually enjoyed my play of it and would play again. 

You're trying to put down groups of tiles and score them, but you can't let them be taken over before you do. The tiles follow a hierarchy of which tiles are dominant to which other ones. However, the trick of the game is that the hierarchies can be changed and locked in. So you play with one eye on the board and another on the hierarchies. Requires deliberate, thinky play with room for clever offensive and defensive moves. 

Not a lot of talking during this one. Played online and we had to see if people had gotten disconnected or fallen asleep.

Played 1 time with 3 players. I wanted to like this -- I mean REALLY wanted to like this -- but I couldn't do it. Despite its whimsical theme and beautiful graphics, this game was dry as a bone in the Arizona desert. It felt like a Knizia game from the late 90's like T&E (which explains why so many other people like it, and why I dislike it so much.) Can I admit that it is brilliant design? Absolutely! The mechanics are unbelievably well integrated with each other, and the game is finely tuned. I would even go as far as saying the coral theme is well-suited for the game, but the mechanics of the game are so finely balanced and well-tuned that they are the only thing I notice when I played this game. I found much about this game to appreciate on a technical level, but nothing about it to enjoy except the Shrimple and the artwork.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
[Abstract Strategy, Resource Management, Set Collection, Planning] (R&D Edition) [4P:4] Whoever said this game had anything in common with E&T is simply wrong. Other than placing square tiles, they are nothing alike in gameplay and in character. The timing of RE is uninteresting, and like many indy games it could have used more cleaning up and streamlining to make it play better. The clunkiness of trading consumed polyps for polyp cubes or alga cylinders and polyp cubes for polyp tiles is extremely clunky. The sizable player turns wreck whatever momentum the game has. Finally, the theme does not work at all. Who are the players? How do they control a fish, some shrimp and the growth of corals? Are they sea gods? Neptune? Poseidon? Habbakuk? I find no joy in this game. Removed from wishlist. *** UPDATE: Confirming the problems with Reef Encounter. When players aren't just taking cubes and tiles, they're constructing large turns in their heads. The board is totally static in the early game with the shrimp shutting down board activity, and people jumping through hoops to generate consumed polyps, which is just tremendously boring. All of that just to create a resource? Why? I also am hugely perplexed by the ascendancy tiles. This mechanism added a significant fiddliness to the proceedings without adding any real value to the gameplay. So you have to have the thing on the right side to "attack", and you can also have it on a certain side to score more. Because those color corals are more dominant? And this can change after the fact (post digestion by the fish)? Oooookay. And perhaps worst of all, the timing and rhythm of the game is abysmal. It's a struggle for the players to keep their minds on the game. RE is one of the bigger game disappointments in terms of my anticipation to actual experience comparison. I have a feeling that RE could have been improved with a lot more development to file down the many rough edges in its design. Too bad.

******* Recommended by my Geekbuddies: 11 ratings, 7.91 Average Rating. Well, now that Z-Man is going to reprint Reef Encounter, and given my satisfaction with their handling of Santiago, I can safely put this on my To Buy List. However, it had better not cost $100!

RATING: 8
Reef Encounter is a tough clam to crack. The innocent, cutesy theme hides a pretty intense knife fight on the ocean floor. Direct conflict, resource management, and timing issues are the components which, looking at them in isolation, are fairly easy to grasp. In combination, however, they prove quite challenging. The final outcome of a game of Reef Encounter seems to be the result of a flock of butterflies flapping their wings (or maybe I should say jellyfish swaying their tentacles in order to not destroy the imagery). How to get them to sway in a way that benefits you is not something that is immediately apparent after the first play. An opaque and highly unusual game.

RATING: 9
Received 12/13/04. Played first game on 12/16/04. Have now played ten games and like it better with each play. After the first play, all four players wanted to play it again. Certainly a brain-burner, and the first game was about 1/2 done before we quit having to reference the rule book. Not one that is simple to teach/learn. But it plays far more easily than it is to learn. A bit of down-time if you play with the analytical. My third play was with 2 players and it was just as enjoyable IMO. Fourth and fifth play was with 3. So far, I see little difference re: number of players. Just as fun, no matter how many play.
Second play required only a couple of minutes of review and it all came back. Played a bit quicker and was a lot of fun. It may be an illusion, but it seems that there's a lot more depth than I'm currently seeing in this one. 
Fourth play was very quick! Around 45 minutes. We all three had played before and there was little down time. But most of my games involve at least one person who has never played the game, slowing it a bit.

RATING: 8
Yes! It turns out Reef Encounter is quite a good game. Solid mechanics and a theme that you won't find anywhere else, which is so refreshing. Some rule peculiarities here and there (necessary, but add convolution). It takes a bit to figure out how everything fits together, but once you do, it is clear that there is a strong system at play here. A relatively straight-forward game to play but a brain burner for sure. Trying to play this briskly means strategic mistakes will be made. Each turn requires thought for optimal play. I think I would like this as a 3-player game, but 4-players was perfectly workable (except for turn wait and less control over what is going on). A nice, mid-weight game.

RATING: 6

My early playings have been with less than enthusiastic partners so in a way has stunted any real attempt at garnering a real feel for this Breese title. Here I thought wooden shrimp would provide for an eagerness to discover what lies within this cardboard box, but instead have only seemed to scare people off with an idea of a game being to deeply abstract.  what to do?  Any interested players out there in South Florida?

Update: it's a brain burner, and the components are beautiful, I just think I am more compelled to play other games.

Damn. I don't know what to think. Game's good BUT - the downtime when you want to calculate most best move is horrible. EDIT - 06.2011
After - totally - 30 plays at SpielByWeb and 3 in real. This one burned out a bit for me. Too static, too defensive, too random (draw of the tiles+cubes), too long with new players (and with them too much of kingmaking). It's not like the game's totally bad. It's still one of the best, but I can't see anything new anymore here for me. Additionally - due to my online experience - I'm usually bored with real players or see them making obvious moves. I'd much rather play Caylus or Tigris&Euphrates. Maybe - unobtainable in polish shops - expansion would change my mind. This way - I'll rather stuck with playing RE online, only.
Nevertheless, thank you, Chris Burm, for my real first love in bordgaming hobby - the Reef Encounter. :)
And for those reading my longish comment: don't hesitate to aks me for a game online, I'll never turn back a game this way.

RATING: 8
My first game, with 4 players, was ridiculously long.  It took over 3 hours to teach and play.  However, by the end, we were playing quickly.  Definitely playable in under 90 minutes with experienced players.  We all waited too long to consume coral, as we didn't yet understand the strategy involved.  It's a beautiful game to look at, has a very fun theme, and has come as close to a perfect theme-mechanics connection as I've seen. 

With 2 players, it becomes a quick one.  I can see this playable in 30 - 45 minutes with 2 players.  It loses some angst, but becomes a good race to consume four times first.   

My rating for this game has climbed the last two times played.  I love it so much as a 2 player game, I'm almost scared to play it with 3 or 4.

This is probably the only game I've ever regretted trading away or selling.  I miss it!

RATING: 4.5
I don't like how the person who triggers the game end is at a disadvantage because his opponents (especially the player to his right) get to score coral fields that never get attacked at only one point less than those scored during the game. This gives everyone incentive not to be the person who ends the game, and therefore the game drags on after it has worn out its welcome, turning into a game of chicken.

I like most of the other parts of the game, and may try tweaking it at some point.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
'Brain-burner' is an over-used phrase which this game certainly earns.  Breese has created a tile-laying game with lots of complicated angles, area-control/activation issues, and the setup and timing of limited scoring opportunities.

The basic units of economy, the polyp tile and the larva cube, are what drive and allow all this to occur, and they also form the basic scoring unit, with values determined by relative strength and locked in place by alga cylinders.

There are ten basic actions, some of which are repeatable, others not, but which are all available during each player turn.

The interplay of tiles taken, area-control and continuing acquisition of activation/control tokens, never minding guiding the scoring the way you want it are what make this a fascinating game.

I am starting this at a 7 on one play; I'm sure I haven't a terrific notion of how to play this after only one game, but I do know I like it and want to try it again, soon.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: N/A

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
This is a brain-burner, and not for the faint of heart. The mechanics are utterly confusing, and you may as well resign your first game to being a learning experience. It will take several plays to "understand" the flow of this game. Despite being given 10 different actions, most turns players are limited to executing 1 or 2 plays. Most of the time players are collecting items for the big play afterwards. The confusion arises from trying to visualize how these 10 different actions string together to yield you victory points. You could take a basic approach by simply collecting and growing your patches of coral. However, it is counter-intuitive to consume one of your own coral to get the all-important consumed polyp tiles, which greatly opens up your play options.

The game seems to combine a number of mechanics together for an intense game of area-control and resource collection. The area-control is reminiscent of Tigris & Euphrates, which hints of Oasis, while the resource collection is just like Le Havre. The novelty is that there is a dynamic coral hierarchy that determines what types of polyps 'dominate' other types, and thus allow consumption (aka attacks) of coral from other players or from your own.

The rules are terribly written, with confusing game terminology. It talks about things like algae cylinders and open-sea areas, but does not point to what they are, or where they are on the board. Thus, players not familiar with this game will be somewhat lost with the rules. Once the rules are understood, the game play is relatively fast. However, the strategy continues to be fascinatingly deep, with layers of tactics in between. Does not make a good gateway game, and is not something that can be quickly taught to newbies--rules took 30-45 minutes to explain. 4P play took 90 minutes. It's worth the investment, but without a veteran's guiding hand, this could easily frustrate and turn-off newcomers.

RATING: 9
Although I now own a physical (signed!) copy of this game, I have still played most of my games of this online at Spiel by Web, and I would say that it is perfectly suited to online play because each turn is meaty.  The first few games can really bog you down with AP, leaving your more decisive opponents in "downtime" while you try to understand the influence you have over the dynamic interaction of different corals.

I look forward to giving it more plays in the future.  In particular, I would like to answer the following question: which do I prefer, Reef Encounter or Tigris and Euphrates?  In the mean time, take the fact that I am even considering this question as an indicator of how good Reef Encounter is.

Note: I read/heard on a blog somewhere someone asking "why do people always compare this to T&E?  It shares more in common with Imperial".  This is a question, with a a very obvious answer: RE and T&E are excellent tile laying games!  However, the point is totally valid: mechanically there is a strong parallel between Imperial and Reef Encounter because both have a layer on top of the area-based play which can be manipulated and which affects the value of your final position.  Still, they are vastly different games, and I recommend Reef Encounter to anyone who enjoys meaty Euros.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Reef Encounter is one of the most original - or perhaps "least derivative" is more accurate - German-style games I have played in a long time (or so it seems after a couple of plays). At first it is difficult to know how to tackle the game, although careful placing of polyp tiles and shrimps is clearly a good idea as it helps to protect your corals from attack. The game is relatively lengthy, but does not outstay its welcome as there are tactical considerations to dwell upon every turn. The most innovative mechanic is the use of coral tiles to determine the relative strength of corals during the game AND to determine the score per polyp tile at the end of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7

The reason this works is that initial coral fields are not terribly static, and you can sway colors by eating your own fields particularly in the end game where you are likely to have larger coral stacks in reserve.

RATING: 8
Area control:tile laying:market manipulation:set collection:

An extremely well themed game that is very analytical so more of a 'gamer's game'. Can be a little tricky to learn but it flows nicely once it gets going.

Funny for a well themed game the play is almost dry as the table is quite quiet with everyone doing their own analysis, that is until the screwage effect kicks in. It is prone to AP but with 3 players you have just enough time to plan and calculate before it is your turn, albeit someone's move may change your plan so flexibility is required. 

There is a relation to T&E here as when coral is growing and attacking it is similiar to Kingdoms in T&E. I do think this feels just a little more analytical and T&E feels just a little more dynamic. The 'market' that is present in this title however makes this a very different game and it is one of the keys to winning as well. 

Timing is the big critical aspect in this game; when to lay down your tiles or hold out to put down more, when to attack a neighboring coral, when to gobble up your coral or push your luck to gobble up more, when to flip over tiles in the market which can help attacking and when to lock them down for points at the end.  

A nice mix of strategic and tactical thinking, I think 3 is the sweet spot for this, more downtime and chaos with 4 and not as much competition with 2 but very scalable all the same and I can see where there might be much more control with only 2 (again like T&E here IMO).  
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 7
2012-11-01

Initial Rating: 7.5 (October 2012)

What a surprise! Great game. The theme is somewhat boring but the gameplay is excellent. This is my kind of game, a brain burner with very low luck :devil:. The only downside? some people can take a long time thinking (long downtime) so perhaps it is a good idea to play it with only 3 players (or only 2).

The components are ok, the art is pretty but not to my personal liking BUT the mechanics are great. They are easy to teach. You can do 10 different actions each turn so you have a lot to consider. There is market value manipulation, area control, set collection and lots of direct conflict. Thank to this, the game offers a plethora of hard decisions through all the game, each action is important, definitively not a game for newcomers, this is a true gamer´s game.

The designer, Richard Breese, was below my radar but I will begin paying him more attention.

I would have never played this due to the uninteresting theme, but I’m glad a friend introduced it to me.

This game is very hard to master, so I see myself playing it again soon because the game is very deep and I have only beginning to scratch its surface :D

Current Rating: 7.5

RATING: 7
Initial rating.  I enjoyed my first play today, but the learning curve is steep enough that I still will feel new to the game the next time I play.  So, I could either really like it, or not.

The fluid nature of the coral building is both appealing and daunting.  To do it well (i.e. quickly) takes quite a bit of foresight and planning of rather complex mechanics.  This coupled with the overall simplicity of the game presents a pleasing (at least on the first play) paradox.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
I was looking forward to playing this game before buying a copy, so when one of our club members brought his copy, I had to play.  I have since played a number of games on SpielByWeb and the results of most of the games has left me a little cold.  The game quickly deteriorates into "if my parrotfish eats a coral each turn and I have a shot at winning... I'll do it."  Eat a coral, use an algae cylinder to lock a tile... repeat until done.

Games just seem to get started and they are over (I've never seen a game go more than 10 or 11 rounds).  No one wants to fall too far behind, so everyone does the same thing over and over again.

Tile draws are critical too... if a triplet becomes available to you, you almost have the game won by taking it.  I like randomness, but this almost breaks the game for me.  There seems to always be a "best move" for each player and no one wants to take risks... at least in all the games I've played so far.

Maybe I just don't "get it"... but judging from the people I've played with, no one else does either.  Scores for every game have been so lopsided, it looked like the last place finisher wasn't even trying.  When everyone is doing their best and if the game is balanced... you would think that you'd never see scores with a 55 point difference, yet it's happened on at least two different occasions.

Because of the apparent balance problems (the starting polyp tiles don't seem to address this at all), Z-Man could have done some re-development on the game before they re-published it, but they didn't.  Playing with the variant rules is a MUST! And restricting Action 1 would be a good addition too... limit Action 1 until round 5 or so or make it so that a player cannot take Action 1 on two consecutive turns.  The objective here is to lengthen the game slightly and make scoring much more tactical.  Removing the score multipliers would help too... make the larvae tiles additive to polyps instead.  Scores would be much closer and game tension would be a lot higher.  Needs a little tweaking... especially with only two or three players.

I finally bought the 2nd edition of the game.  Maybe a little more face to face play will help make some sense out of what is going on... but for now the game only rates a 6.

RATING: 8
R&D Games edition.  A tactical game of (underwater) resource management and area control.  It appears that, at first glance, a significant amount of end-game control is obtained through establishing a firm 'lock' on various coral tiles by way of placing certain colored algae cylinders earlier (but not too early) in the game.  The 'flipping' nature of these coral tiles causes enough chaos to the ever changing board composition and requires re-evaluating each turn.  The real trick is to manipulate the direction of the game to fit your earlier intensions.  To grow or to eat... if it were only that simple.

RATING: 9
The grandpappy of the more complex tile-laying Euro family, Reef Encounter is maybe more fun for me because it fits my brain almost perfectly - I haven't lost all that much since I got the hang of it. But I think there's enough in here to keep anyone busy. There's a stock game, there's some very tactical tile-laying, and any canny player has to think carefully about whether to go for points early or late. 

As far as production values, the rules are pretty awful, and could've been written in four pages instead of twenty. On the other hand, I have to give these guys mad props for putting together a game with no language on the tiles that's still pretty intuitive. I'd like to see more high-end Euro games with Reef Encounter's ultra-evocative design aesthetic.

RATING: N/A
Reef Encounter is about life on a coral reef! Using polyp tiles, players grow different types of corals, which they can protect from being attacked by other corals through judicious placing of their four shrimp counters. To be successful players must consume polyps from neighboring corals in order to acquire the 'consumed' polyp tiles that are the key to the game. The consumed polyp tiles have a myriad of uses (and have a similar effect to the action points in games like Tikal and Java). Most importantly they can be used to flip over or lock the coral tiles, which determine the respective values of the different types of coral at the end of the game.

RATING: 4

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
* Game play:

Nice, original (?) elements: the pattern of dominance between polyps is set during the game by the players. This creates a kind of stock exchange where players invest (use algae) in order to influence the "market".

I get a feel of ebb and flow, and of slow changes, with this game. For instance, when you eat coral, you get victory points; but on the other hand, you're left with fewer tactical possibilities, since you also lose (eat) a shrimp in the process. Or when you use an alga and change the order of dominance among corals, not all "dominance flips" are good for you.

- Game weight:

Game weight being an important criterion for my wife and I, here are some thoughts:

There is a lot to think about, in this game; I like this, as there is no time to get bored. The whole dominance chain is not obvious (10 pairwise dominance tiles) and constantly changes, the geometry of the reef is important, what the parrot fish of your opponents have eaten is important to memorize, etc. Resolving actions is fast, and we spend almost all our time thinking. Yummy!

After playing 4 times, we have not really "got" the game yet. Yet, we do not feel completely lost, and have a lot of tactics and strategies to think about? only to discover later than we have very naive ideas.  However, I often feel like I'm flailing about in water: I don't get a clear picture of the longer term consequences of my actions.  Especially since their is an ebb and flow of dominance, in the game; it feels like you can't gain much in one turn (you can make a big coral, but have to wait for your turn to eat it, and it can be damaged while you're waiting); when you make points by eating coral, you also lose a shrimp, and hence protection for other corals; when you lock a dominance tile, other tiles flip, and this is most of the time not to your full benefit.

The rules are actually not very complex in themselves: they are easy to remember, well summarized in the provided cheat sheet, with details that are easily found in the rule book. They are more complex than Small World's / Samurai, but maybe less complex than those of Race for the Galaxy, and certainly way simpler than those of Commands & Colors: Ancients.

Thus, we enjoyed Reef Encounter since our first game (since the rules are simple enough, it is easy to play with them in our head and imagine tactics and strategies). And we look forward to playing more games, as we feel that there is more to understand and discover.

Our games take 1 hour to 1h30, and we like to take all the time we need for thinking.

Reef sometimes feels a little bit like Go: it is easier to defend corals of the right shape.

Reef Encounter leaves me wonder how Go would fare with my wife and I: it is also abstract, offers a lot to think about, has simpler rules,... Maybe Reef has the advantage of being shorter?

* Theme: The theme is well explained in the rule book, and many of the mechanics are quite thematic: polyp larva float in the open sea, you need a larva to start conquering the reef, killed polyps "contain" algae and larvae (they can be exchanged for these), shrimps protect corals,... Some mechanics are not so thematic, though, as far as I can see: the open see contains (adult) polyps (which you fetch at the end of your turn) that can end up on the reef; you can only take 4 polyps from behind your screen in order to grow them on the reef: why?...

* Rules:

The rule book (Quined Games edition) is fine, but is not as clear as many other rulebooks. It goes deeper and deeper in the details (first level: overview; then quick descriptions, with some key concepts left for the third level [attacks, shrimps, eating,…]). This method does not quickly give a concrete understanding of how the game plays.

Advice: the parts in blue *should not be read at first*: they significantly add to the length of the text, and do not help grasping the rules.

Playing the game is actually quite simple. The thematic introduction helps.

I haven't yet seen anything not covered quite explicitly by the rules.

* Bits: not the highest quality, but very good, still.

Some problems with the bits (I'm nitpicking, as the following to impeded in any way the game play):
- Tiny scratch on one reef board
- One shrimp is one-eyed
- white cubes have traces of dirt/color
- Wooden shrimps: some slightly chipped sides
- The typography in the rule book is a little heavy for my taste, though, with very narrow columns that make reading more difficult.

RATING: 7.9
Richard Breese knows how to make unique and original games.  Underneath the surface is an economics/stock manipulation abstract game, vaguely reminiscent of Acquire.  This is disguised by the "theme" of the game.  Personally I don't care much for the look/art/theme, and abstract games are generally not my favorite genre;  However, the uniqueness of the innovative and solid game mechanics of this game make it a pleasant surprise.  Rules are fairly straightforward once you start playing, but they are far from intuitive -- every time I take this game off the shelf I have to reread the rulebook and almost make an effort to relearn the game.  As a result I am unlikely to suggest it on a whim and between the odd rules and uninteresting appearance the game does not get played as often as it could.

RATING: N/A
Beautiful game and so much fun to play - added plus that the theme ties into my other main hobby.  

Tough decisions on when to play a tile color - should I play it now so I don't waste time or should I wait until my opponent grows a weaker color so I can eat part of it?  Also, timing is important in locking in the dominance of the coral tiles.  Tons of decisions each turn - many of them can be agonizing!  You need to think ahead a few turns and then hope your opponents don't screw you over.  You can analyze everything or play by instinct - but either way this is not a light game and will take a few plays to grasp the basic strategies.  Hard to describe the feeling you get when you pull off an amazing move - that's what puts this game over the top for me.  I can't get enough!

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Excellent combination of strategy and tactics game. The ideal heavy 2-player thinker in 90 minutes. I already dislike the 3-player game due to increased AP, and outright avoid playing this with 4 because of the multiplayer chaos in addition to even more AP.
But with just 2, man, is this great. Completely different from any other of the usual Euro-stuff, it will take time for this game to sink in and give away its secrets. the gameplay is fully non-linear, without snowballing and such. short-term tactics are essential to optimize resource security to execute a mid-term plan, after which the long-term strategy takes over as you gear towards ending the game.
All this allows for plenty of screwage, making completely unexpected moves, setting up decoys for your opponent, half-way through change of plans, and many more ideas solely limited by the creativity of the players. The only random elements (starting tiles and open-sea larva-tile combinations) secure replayability whilst avoiding standard opening moves, and are not at all unpleasantly luck-based: Especially the open-sea tiles are placed sequentially, fully allowing players to plan ahead of things and set up their plays. Wonderful.
However, be prepared to play around 10 games before you have a decent grasp on what is going on, and have an idea of the sheer possibilities involved - much more than your average game. The first few games will consist of messing around and simply getting a feel for what the mechanics can do. As such, players are not focussing and this will lengthen the game (150 minutes is not strange). Do however allow yourself to get past that point, and enjoy the utter greatness that is Reef Encounter.

RATING: 8
Reef Encounter is a heavy tile laying/set collection game that has players vying control of various reefs. Players take turns playing “polyp tiles” and changing the landscape of the different reefs all in the effort to feed their Parrot Fish, which counts as points at the end of the game.  

Did I mention this game is heavy? Yes, there are lots of big strategic decisions and some brain burning maneuvering. Also, when playing for the first time, be prepared to wade through the not-so-clearly-written rules. It will be confusing! If you’re still up for the challenge I HIGHLY recommend many of the quick-start guides or rule summaries to help you on your maiden voyage into the reef. With all that said, this game is quite the catch! There are many possible actions a player may take during his/her turn and you must be able to think-out your actions in advance. Highly recommended for hard-core players who like heavy strategic games with lots of tough choices and decisions.

RATING: 6
The downtime is a bit of a problem in this brain burning Euro-style game.  Many elements to think about and keep track of, not the least of which is scoring that changes depending on the actions of you and your fellow players.  You might set up for a big move on your next turn, and by the time it rolls around back to you, twenty minutes have passed and the board looks completely different, requiring a lengthy reassessment of the situation.  For those who don't mind this, it's really a quality game, and I personally enjoy it.  But it's tough to find players that really appreciate that sort of game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Reef Encounter does share some basic mechanisms with [boardgame=42][/boardgame] (claiming ownership of territory, no tile color is "your" color, consuming four tiles ~ creating a monument), but ultimately these are very different games. Tigris & Euphrates' dramatic, decisive swings are replaced by a subtle investment game; instead of eying a dramatic external conflict to make up ground in a color you're behind in, you'll can devalue that color so you don't need to catch up. Despite enjoying the brain burning aspects of this game, I find it painfully convoluted and unintuitive. While I'm up for the challenge, it's just not as enjoyable as other equally challenging games.

RATING: 7
Mechanically simpler than the rulebook suggests but there is a lot of depth and complexity in the tactical choices. What makes my brain hurt is that players have a lot of discretion in how they order their actions in their turn with many internal interconnections, and getting it wrong can be fatal. But it's such a engrossing game, especially when deciding when and where to start eating your shrimp! I can't see me playing this other than online, so I can really ponder my moves without making my fellow gamers wait. Would be excellent on a tablet (iOS) platform. Face to face sessions would have too much downtime while each player updates their strategy and make their decisions for the turn. Reef Encounter reminds me of Tigris & Euphrates but I like Reef Encounter more. Overall, Reef Encounter is a game I respect for its design but doubt I'd play it frequently.

RATING: 6
I've only played this once so far so I'm neither an expert nor am I saying my rating will stay at this level after further plays but as of now it's a 6.

I went into the game with an open mind and actually thought the game was going to be very good as I prefer "thinky" Euro games such as this. It definitely has a lot to think about as you attacks corals, eat corals, flip coral tiles, obtain alga tubes, obtain polyps etc. Each round you have 10 actions (although one option is doing nothing) so there is always something to think about as the board evolves and changes every round. The rules as many have noted aren't that intuitive to grasp as the game play is somewhat circular in nature.. Do A to obtain B.. With B you can do C...when you do C you can then do D... Once D is done you get points for E... etc

It's all a bit complex as you first try to formulate an idea of what strategies will work best at realizing the penultimate goal of having the highest scores by your parrot fish consuming the most valuable corals throughout the game.

So if the game has lot's of decision making and room for strategy/tactics... and I am predisposed to liking those types of Eurogames... then why didn't I like Reef Encounter on our first play? Well, I can't say for certain as I don't have enough experience yet but for one thing... the game feels somewhat repetitive and fiddly. It's not that it's overly complex... just that you seem to have to go around in circles a lot to get things done and there's a lot of cube and tile moving and removing and flipping over and back again every turn. Maybe I'm reaching a stage where "cube confusion" is settling but it just seems like this is one of the more abstract thematically rich games in the Euro genre.

The game certainly has a good theme and it does come through in the gameplay quite well... and the graphic design of the game is quite nice to look at. It all still does feel abstract though as we push cubes around and flip tiles.

For now though I'm certainly willing to give this more tries and my rating certainly may climb from the 6 level... but I'm being as honest as I can be in saying that so far... Reef Encounter just doesn't really float my boat yet. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 10
Reef Encounter is a fantastic ecosystem of intertwined mechanisms. It's basically a tile placement game with the added challenges that several steps are required each time you want to earn points and both the strength and the values of the tiles can be manipulated by the players.

To succeed, you have to plan several turns ahead, both yours and your opponents', and you can never rest as you literally have to eat your "economic engine" and start over again to continue to score. Add to that a beautiful and colorful board and you have a close to perfect game.

RATING: N/A
Players take on the role of shrimp and parrotfish. Play down polyps (represented by tiles of 5 different colors) and eat them with your shrimp to earn VPs. Polyps can eat other polyps to give you options during your turn, and polyps can change in the food chain order, which players can permanently fix, and enhance the score of the tiles you have eaten. Many options on your turn and very engaging.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Only played with 2, but it's still great.  Would love to try more players.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 4
This game has a lot of thinking going into a single point scoring action.  Points are only scored 4 times in the game per player, and not necessarily at the same time.  With multiple players the game can be quite brutal.  Starts off pretty slow, and the game is extremely abstract.  The artwork is really good, the theme is ok.  The actions seems clunky and people are subject to Analysis Paralysis throughout the game.  You have to think as high as 4-5 actions ahead in order to pull something off.  That can cause a lot of AP.  Consumed polyps are key to the game, to such a degree that it is really what the game is about (not the parrotfish consumption, but coral fighting other coral).  A lot of business on the board and the Open Sea Board.  Would like to try it with 4 players to see if my opinion of it changes.

RATING: 9
This is a pretty simple game to learn. The actions are straight forward and are entwined with other actions, but WOW what a mind bender. I played this with three new players and we were all at one time or another literally scratching our heads about what action to take and what to do.  Everything is so subtly connected it was amazing trying to figure out what tactic to apply.

Second game was even more interesting than the first. The depths this games requires in order to accomplish good scores asks the players to monitor so much information and figure out the algorithm that makes one decision better in the projected long term. Only the long term can be altered very easily by competitors unless precautions are taken.

RATING: 9
Gamer's game with a terrible rulebook... could not learn it the first time around. All the different pieces and terminology: polyp tiles, corals, alga cylinders, larva cubes, parrotfish, shrimp, every page a different dimension and layer. It all comes together into a cohesive game with a really cool and dynamic set of dominance rules among the five colors of coral. I get it and I like it; there's a surprising amount of combat and interaction given the marine wildlife theme, yet the balance and feel is very euro. Shout out to Dice Tower review with Ryan Metzler for helping our learning group make sense of the rules.
