RATING: 4
TRY Nov/05: Unbelievable learning curve; way too complex with unfamiliar & repetitive terminology.  Coral boards, coral tiles, polyps, consumed polyps, larva coral, algae cylinders.  Add in player colours matching (or not) cube colours matching (or not) algae colours... Ugh!

Too many mechanics; doesn't really match the underwater theme.  Feels very abstract when I played (on spielbyweb.com), and very fiddly.  Seemed weird to have just 5-7 turns, and very BIG turns (while everyone else just sits & waits).  I found it very uninteresting, overall.

Sure, there are ways to control the scoring, and you can try to limit the other players by eating their unprotected corals.  But with the shrimp protecting up to 5 coral tiles, and with 5 tiles being good enough to score points, this seems to prevent offensive plays (ie. creativity) by making defense too easy.

I'll play if someone else wants to, but in no way would I see the point of owning this, nor trying to teach others how to play.  The payoff isn't worth it.  Was HIGH on my wishlist, and is now removed.

RATING: 9
I love this game. It is an absolute favorite, every time I've played it (four so far) I increase its rating a little. The ruleset is very straightforward but it can be tricky to play well, never in a frustrating way, but such that the game offers a sense of accomplishment when you do coordinate a series of strong moves.

My favorite aspect is the manipulation of the coral dominance tiles for points and for allowing you to consume tiles into resources.

It's owned by a friend, and I play it about every 8 months on average, but I think about this game more than any game I own.

Its more for fans of economic speculation games then for those interested in marine biology.

RATING: 7.9

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
This one has grown on me with each play. 

In the first time I played I realized there was a lot of stuff to pay attention to but I wasn't able to plan or execute anything useful. Usually, in a given turn you do parts of a larger plan that will come together two or three turns away. If you don't know what you're trying to do, it's hard to understand why you should do anything on your turn. That's why the game's so hard in the first couple of plays.

Also, having tiles in front of your screen is crucial. If you're not able to get some, you'll be severely limiting your options because you'll be unable to manipulate the stock market. And the market is the core of the game. New players tend to forget this and miss a big part of the fun.

After a couple of plays everything clicks in.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9.3
Apparently Richard Breese got the idea for this game from watching a Blue Planet special on coral reefs.  It has parrotfish and shrimp and algae and coral varieties with shifting dominance relationships to one another that vie for territorial dominance by eating each other.  This game has one of the strangest dynamics of ebb and flow that actually feels like some bizarre underwater ecosystem.  It is brain bending and inspirational, and we always have a great time discovering new ways to devour your own and others coral for personal benefit.  Nothing is permanent in Reef Encounter, until you fix the dominance relationship of two coral varieties with an algae disk!

Nothing like it, and one of my favorite games of all time.

RATING: 5
[ 2 3 ] 4 joueurs
45 minutes par joueur

Gros jeu et ambiance très sérieuse. Malgré le thème, un jeu très abstrait aux règles complexes, dont certaines artificielles car ajoutées pour interdire les stratégies dégénérées.

Les mécanismes sont excessivement tarabiscotés et parfois frustrants. Pratiquement, on ne peut construire un territoire qu'en capturant ses propres pièces (celles des adversaires sont toujours protégées), parfois même en utilisant les pièces adverses pour cela. Weird!

Pourtant si l'on survit à la lecture des règles et à la première partie (largement plus déconcertante que Tigre et Euphrate), on découvre un jeu pas inintéressant. Trop tarabiscoté toutefois pour que j'en fasse l'un de mes favoris où que je le fasse découvrir à tout mon entourage.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Fairly abstract and dry tile laying game. I tend to like games like this, and this game offers some interesting choices. you grow out your corals and try to protect them before eating them. The bigger they get, the more points they are worth when eaten, but the harder they are to protect. The value of what you've eaten can go up or down depending on the orientation of tiles on a main board that depict what types of corals beat which other types.

It gets complicated quickly, and it's hard to know when you've made the right choice. I thought it was interesting but somewhat anti-climactic at the end. Other players who usually enjoy dryer abstracts with me, thought this game was a little too dry.

:nostar: Art and components
:star: Innovative mechanics
:nostar: Handles 5 or more players
:star: Good with min or max players
:star: Low time between turns
:star: Comfortable complexity
:star: Re-playability
:star::nostar::nostar: Enjoyment
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

An intriguing game which has quite a lot of visual appeal despite the thin theme. The game play is a tad dry unpolished (The reference sheet has an "Action 8" which is "not to take an action"), but becomes more intriguing as you go along. I found a bit too much going on for my taste and it is real feat of concentration to keep track of all the factors that are playing into the final score, lots of mental bookkeeping, which is not a bad thing if that is what you are into, but it is easy to loose track and trail behind if you do not pay attention all the time. I understand that this is the whole point of the game but my point is that it might not be for everyone and it feels like a competitive puzzle/sandbox game with all of the dynamics being the other players (mostly). Still, a good game and if played within a group of balanced playing strength it is quiet satisfying. 
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 6
ONE OF THE WORST RULE BOOKS EVER. This lost a rating point just for that.

Relatively straightforward game despite the awful rules. Grow your clumps. Attack clumps to get important special powers essential for scoring and determining what can attack what. The game converges cleverly as you have to eventually wipe out your groups to score them.

Lots of AP, but the AP I don't like: The kind of time wasted in seeing what moves are possible.

Not as interactive as Tigris and Euphrates, the game to which it's often compared. Much of the interaction is more by necessity rather than bashing the leader.

RATING: 5
A very, very heavy ruleset for an abstract game.  To me, a heavy ruleset is fine if it's linked by an appropriate theme.  An abstract game is fine if the ruleset is simple.  But heavy rules in an abstract game are no fun to figure out!  Nothing is intuitive!  And even though things *almost* come together in a very neat way, and even though there are some truly clever moves possible in this game, your plans fall apart 99% of the time with more than 2 players.  It's a game where your ability to do that great, incredible thing depends on everyone else not trying to stop you for a turn.  You have to luck into that.  This would be fine if it worked as a tactical game.  It doesn't.  Like Power Grid, accumulation of points involves coordination of a multi-stage process--collecting coral, placing coral on the board, consuming coral, and locking dominance.  Unlike Power Grid, you have zero ability to coordinate if your opponents realize what you're up to, and they always do!

However, it does work as a 2-player game. With only 2, you can follow-through that brilliant plan of yours!  But since the game is so rules-heavy and abstract, it's too much work for the payoff.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Reef Encounter tells the story of life and death on a coral reef. Players share the same board space, and they can all control the growth and expansion of coral, but you do not necessarily play a single type. In fact, you must strive to score in as many types of coral as possible, with the caveat that certain types may score higher than others. Those corals that score higher, are the types that are more powerful, and the types that are more powerful are same types that you can place to consume the weaker coral, which then contribute to your final score, but the points you obtain from scoring those coral are dependent on...wait, wasn't I just here?

Therein lies the genius of Richard Breese's design. There is a certain "circle-of-life" quality here, where players must wrestle with discovering where exactly they can fit amidst its processes. Somehow, Reef Encounter manages to convey life on a coral reef as if it were a battlefield. Coral strength changes throughout the game, and through clever manipulation of the strength tiles, your parrotfish may be able to eat vast areas of coral for a hefty sum of points. However, "your" (and the quotes are intentional) areas are never fully safe from harm, as swathes of coral unprotected by your shrimp (i.e. the Guardians of the Sea) are prone to be consumed by stronger coral, or worse, eaten by other players' parrotfish for their own gain.

Reef encounter comes off as an abstract game, but from the way Mr. Breese describes the premise, its mechanisms do seem to parallel, to some extent, the ecosystem of a coral reef--they create interactions so interwoven and so organic that they must have been intentionally designed. The player interaction here is so brutal it is clinical, there is depth present nearly as boundless as the sea, and these concepts are all encapsulated in a cohesive set of mechanisms. Marvelous!
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 5
08-03-2015. Rated after 1 play. Reef Encounter is a game for 2-4 players in which you play 'fish' eating coral. The game ends if a player as eaten his 5th coral reef OR when all reef values are locked in place. Value of reef is determined by which color reef dominates over other colors.

Each turn, you may do many actions, some several times. First, you determine if your fish eats bits of coral. Subsequently, you may grow reefs if you spend a cube in the color of the reef and have reef tiles of that color in your supply. If you ever replace other reef tiles, you get those in supply. You are then able to use those tiles to get cubes, which you can use to lock and change the value of those reefs. At the end, your last action is to pick a couple of new tiles and a cube.

I did think Reef Encounter is an good game. It's mechanically incredibly solid to my understanding after playing it once. But it feels like you are making many though decisions, but in the end you play a fish eating coral and manipulating values. There is no theme here, nor soul in my book.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
----------

I really enjoy this game... it's a bit like Tigris with an abstracted stock market that determines both coral power and end game victory points.  It is a strategic game, but it becomes more tactical as more players are added; I love this game with 3 (definitely the sweet spot)... it's also great with 2, but not nearly as good.  

I honestly wish the boards were bigger, which I think would lead to more conflict.  But, I do enjoy the tension as you grow your reef, because once you exceed a certain size, you open yourself up to attacks.  

I also like the invaluable "eaten polyp tiles."  As a player, you MUST conserve these for later, when locking down tiles is more important, but if you want to unleash an assault on a vulnerable (yet dominant) coral, you can play the cost/benefit of sacrificing a tile for hurting your opponent.  Great decisions to be made here.

I still feel like conflict is more with 3, which I like -- this game isn't much fun if there isn't much conflict.

----------

RATING: 6
Approx weight: 1400 grams - Z-Man games
I actually found the rules very easy to understand when I had all the game pieces in front of me and I skipped over all the purple hint text. (The text is perhaps a little wordy and does try in parts to over explain things.) I found myself engrossed in all the action and evaluating all the options, but at the same time I didn't feel like it ever bogged down or became too much of a brain burner. I liked the scoring where each polyp tile was only worth one point in itself - the other four potential points are on the open sea board. This added an extra dimension to the set collection that is vital to scoring well. Lots of options, downtime not excessive, lots of player interaction, nice theme and bits, reasonably quick game - what's not to like? Well, the game relies on each player pulling their weight to a certain extent to keep the other players in check. Especially in a four player game when a couple of the players aren't doing this the game becomes frustrating. There's only so much a single player can do to stop another player from running away with it. The frustration is exacerbated by the fact that a low interaction game is just boring. After several plays this game has lost its shine for me and is only something I'll bring out on rare occasions - never with more than three.

RATING: 7

This is not for people who don't like confrontation...luckily I do.

RATING: 8
Z-man version
Reef Encounter is a very well-conceived themed abstract.  Players build corals of five different varieties (colors) on bare rock.  A coral is claimed, and partially protected, by placing a shrimp on it.  Additionally, there is the open sea board that shows the fluctuating state of dominance in the coral world.  For example, sometimes Red coral can replace White coral, sometimes vice-versa.  The dominance situation can be changed globally by any player on their turn, but players can also lock in specific dominance states for the rest of the game.  Finally players can feed their own coral, shrimp and all, to their parrotfish.  It is the coral tiles in the parrotfish that are counted at the end of the game.

There are 10 actions a player may take on a turn, some of them repeatedly.  This presents many options (on some turns) to drastically affect the board setup and mess with your opponents' plans.  Thus, experienced players will play defensively, so as not to expose unprotected coral tiles for their opponents' consumption.  This tendency for defensive play can lead to some extended pondering, which could lead to some dragging.  However, there's something about this game that makes a touch of AP ok.  There are a few rules about attacking coral that provide really interesting options for clever defense.  And the ability to protect and deprotect your coral allows you to recover from an untenable offense, and even consume your own coral with another to enhance your flexibility.  The rock on which the corals are grown have asymmetric holes that don't support coral growth that can both provide natural defense and stymie growth of your coral.

I'm very impressed with this game, but I had to give it a 7.5 because I know I wouldn't always be in the mood for it.  It's quite demanding.  Quickly: plays very well at 2-4, components are fantastic, rules are well done with good examples.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

RATING: 10

RATING: 8
This game is surprising in a couple of aspects.  First of all, there's the theme.  Who would've thought to create a very thematic game about life in a coral reef with game mechanics that are supposedly true to the theme?  Secondly, it's a heavier Euro-style game that I actually enjoyed playing even on the first play.  Now that's saying something!  It helps that the game, while initially quite confusing, seems to fall into place after only a few turns, and it seems to move along at a good clip (or at least I was immersed enough in the game that it felt that way).  The depth of the game is in the strategy, and making sure you plan ahead at least a turn or two.  There is a definite nasty factor in the cutthroat nature of the those killer corals, but I find the direct player interaction to be refreshing in a Euro-style game.  The components are quite nice too (are they called "shreeples" or "shrimpeeples"?), although after the snorkeling I've done in the Caribbean I would have liked to see some more interesting-looking coral.  Just don't introduce humans in the next expansion or that lovely reef will be decimated in no time...  Game design: 4.5/5  Personal enjoyment: 3.5/5  Total: 8/10

RATING: 6
The game's original theme (growing coral reefs in order to feed a parrotfish) isn't really enough to raise it above mediocrity. The decisions you face throughout your turn take an awful amount of thought, especially if you cannot easily match your polyp tiles with your larva cubes, which is the key to winning the game. This means that there's a great deal of strategy needed to play the game well as there's so many things to consider during your turn e.g. which corals are dominant over others, using algae to alter the dominance of the coral tiles, which types of polyps/larva are available to you etc. Because of this it quite often feels as though there's too much thought required in order to be successful. I guess the game just doesn't make those decisions seem important enough to feel worthwhile, which is an obvious failing.

RATING: 7
Reef Encounter is a great game that I'll gladly play on most occasions. It's full of tough choices and there's the constant "frustration" of not being able to do everything you want to on your turn coupled with the anxiety of waiting to see if your hard crafted plans will bear reward. For this, and also for the tile laying, shrimp moving aspects of play, Reef Encounter is reminiscent of Tigris and Euphrates. Reef Encounter can be somewhat abstract in play, but it nevertheless has one of the most elegant integrations of theme with game mechanics that I've had the pleasure to experience. If there's a down side to this game it's that Reef Encounter can get bogged down by players prone to analysis paralysis. I'm very glad to have Reef Encounter as part of my collection. Highly recommended.

Also, we ended with some people eating only two corals (counting the last round) and I think that should have been four for almost everyone.

Next game went SO much better. People were attacking their own coral to get consumed tiles and making larger plays with these tiles later. This playing really showed how good the game is.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 8
The theme is fun. The artwork is gorgeous. Some people compare the game to Tigris & Euphrates. Better informed rumors maintain that Reef Encounter is actually a stock investment game in disguise with enjoyable "take that" tactics. Forget Tigris -- the rumors are correct!

To begin, I think the comparisons to T&E are quite overstated. Timing your plays is more important than positioning, which is limited by the small and disconnected boards. That certain chains of coral can absorb others is less like conflicts in T&E and more like hostile mergers. If anything, it plays more like an elaboration (if you squint) on Acquire. You want to play tiles to the board that help increase the size of your corals, and then you want to make sure you are "invested" in the largest chains. If that doesn't sound like Acquire under the sea, then I don't know what does. Given that Acquire is one of my favorite games, it's all good news here.

Reef Encounter is almost immediately confrontational. Gameplay is fun. And yet I can never shake a certain reservation. Something about the four disconnected reef boards bugs me. They make the game space seem disjointed. Rather than a common map with one large battle for dominance, what you're really competing in is numerous smaller battles. I can understand the limitation on geography, but it also feels inorganic and samey. Finally, the multi-step process for playing pieces also left me with a feeling of inelegance.

I've played Reef encounter a number of times now, with 2, 3, and 4 players. Four is clearly my favorite as it has led to the most confrontation. You are forced to grow your corals next to the others, which in turn leads to more fights. This in turn makes the struggle to invest in the most valuable corals more interesting because the entire board is fragile and subject to interesting swings. When played this way, I love Reef Encounter.

Overall, the game is growing on me, but it is highly dependent on the players. With people who like to nibble away at corals and flip the dominance tiles (i.e., investments), the game is fun. With a more passive group or with fewer players, it's stagnate. I doubt this will be my go to tile laying game, but I do like it.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Still, it's an engaging game, and beautiful as well.

RATING: 7

I sat down to play this game with another newbie and a guy who has "played it 200 times". That was the first clue I was in for trouble. The second clue was when the rules explanation met the board. The strategic placement of multiple coral reefs to protect against invasion wasn't something I got right away. About the time I started understanding that concept, the dominance/scoring tiles started getting flipped and I learned how that can completely change the game. The game started to come to an end and the experienced player had a coral reef on the board that exceeded my entire score and that was only about half his score. It was pretty much a runaway game.

There's a lot to think about in this game. Taking into account what coral can eat other coral and how that can change and who's likely to want to make it change is a lot of brain burny. It was pretty overwhelming in my first play, but I still really enjoyed it. I'm hoping that further plays will make this game come together a little bit more for me and that I can start to feel like I know what I'm doing instead of aimlessly wandering the ocean bed.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6.5

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 6
Seemingly a commodity speculation game, but the commodity values are frequently static, are somewhat divorced from the action on the board (they affect the combat but are not affected by combat), and can change very suddenly. The winner/loser struck me as pretty random.

At the end of your turn you pick from a randomized selection of batches of one cube and up to three tiles. Typically the synergy within the batch is terrible (you need a cube to match tiles you want to place). I may have AP deciding which batch is the least pointless to take. (In theory you could try to look ahead to the future, but it isn't so easy to predict what other players will be doing.)

If you can't get the cube you need to place tiles of a certain color, the other thing you can do is see if you can capture a [i]tile[/i] of the desired color that you could then trade in for a cube. That involves working your way through the logic of the board that shows which colors can currently capture which. Pretty abstract.

On paper there are way too many things you could do on your turn, but I seem to whittle them down without much trouble. On the other hand, I'm pretty sure there are some excellent moves that I just don't perceive, and that kind of irritates me.

The Z-Man components are beautiful, functional, and the required play area is not very large. The game also doesn't take that long to play.

I might like this better without the commodity speculation. In other words: If the goal of the game were to consume the most tiles, period.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
Unique game mechanics. A real brain burner.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 9
[Rated 2005/05/07 based on 1 play]

Tile-laying meets brain-burning strategy.  I suffered terribly from AP, and loved every minute of it.

There's no choice but to plan a number of turns ahead - because of the turn order, the resource relationship between cubes and tiles is quite delayed.  It's fun to plan, and it's very tense waiting to see if you can pull it off without someone else disrupting the final link in the chain.

The coral supremacy chart is brilliant - rock-paper-scissors that each player can tweak to their advantage, and finally lock in place if they play just right.

Also, the wooden shrimps are *so* cute!

If only it wasn't so expensive.  Fingers crossed for a re-print...

RATING: 8.5
After my first play, it was really hard to know how much I liked it because one of the players in my first game was really gushing, and it was hard to seperate his enthusiasm from my own.  After my second play, I was pretty sure.

The rules, at a scanning glance, seem pretty complex, but I had the benefit of a good game explanation and it wasn't a problem at all.  Once you understand the actions on the reference card, you pretty much understand the game.

It is a dynamic game, and every time you score, you give up territory and some of your ability to defend yourself.  This makes an interesting end game situation where a player's weakened defenses force them to prioritize and potentially leave valuable coral unguarded for other players.  There is plenty of room for clever placement, blocking, and sneak attacks.  I wish there was room for a fifth player, though.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

Which is a pity, since there is a nice and difficult game in there.

RATING: 8.5
I don't think this is 'Tigris with a stock market', even though both games do share a certain spatial viciousness. RE's scoring system doesn't reward aggressive play like in Tigris (although consuming polyp tiles is the only way to control the market and ensure you get maximum points). The timing of your actions seems a little more critical in this game, particularly when to feed your parrot fish the first and last times. There's definitely a greater feeling of tactical freedom in Tigris, but RE allows for more long-term planning. I enjoy both - they feel different enough to share shelf space with one another.

RATING: N/A
Meaty tile laying area management game, where you build up coral reefs, then eat them for points when they're large. However, there are 5 different types of tiles, so you want to eating the tiles that will be worth the most at the end of the game. Players struggle against each other, flipping tiles which determine which types are stronger than others, which allows them to eat the reefs of their opponents.

We've only played it once, but it was very enjoyable. It seemed like there was a rhythm to find, where you build up, eat a large amount of tiles, then use that to grow a different reef even larger.

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: N/A
Reef Encounter is about life on a coral reef! Using polyp tiles, players grow different types of corals, which they can protect from being attacked by other corals through judicious placing of their four shrimp counters. To be successful players must consume polyps from neighboring corals in order to acquire the 'consumed' polyp tiles that are the key to the game. The consumed polyp tiles have a myriad of uses (and have a similar effect to the action points in games like Tikal  and Java). Most importantly they can be used to flip over or lock the coral tiles, which determine the respective values of the different types of coral at the end of the game.
=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-

=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-=-
RATING: 7
I am really late to the Reef Encounter party. Now that I have finally gotten to play it, I understand why it was so popular back when it was released. In a lot of ways it feels like Tigris and Euphrates, even though the actual mechanisms for laying tiles and attacking other players are completely different. Like T&E, it is important to build a large area of control, but the bigger you get the more susceptible you are to your opponent's attacks. This creates an interesting push your luck situation, as large reefs can score lots of points, but your opponent's will rarely let them survive long enough to do so.

If I had picked this up five years ago, I would probably have played the crap out of it. Now, I don't really have the opportunity to play this type of game very often and when I do, I simply like Tigris & Euphrates better.

RATING: 7
Despite the general acclaim it received, I found it incredibly hard to find anyone willing to play Reef Encounter due to its steep point of entry.  So while I appreciate the depth with which how the mechanics work with each other, I can't help but wonder if the game is too complicated for its own good.  After all, no matter how good something else, does it really matter if no one is willing to play again?

Reef Encounter featured a strong theme, although I can't vouch as to how accurate it is.  The actions one could take felt rather unintuitive, which no doubt added to the long down time each turn.  The strategy seemed to be building a string of polyp tiles only to be removed later because the more you consume, the more you resources you will have to work with subsequently.  It was a lot more confrontational then I would have liked.  I also felt that towards the end, as the number of your shrimps decrease, your options become more and more limited in what meaningful actions you can take.

Difficult to learn and hard to play well.  I just don't think it has the pay off that one would feel after a long but fruitful session.  So you are left with the sense that you just experienced something that might have been good, but nothing you want to commit the time to again to find out.  Which is a shame.

RATING: 7
Very intense game of trickily interlocking mechanisms.  For your first game or so, you might do well to try it on SpielByWeb, since it constantly shortlists your options to suit the circumstances.  That being said, this is a grueling, but interesting third-party struggle for dominance.  You make the coral grow, possibly consuming other coral over which it has dominance, and claim a coral territory as your own by guarding it with one of your shrimp.  You can manipulate the relative dominance of any pair of coral types, but you must choose the moment when it is most beneficial for you to have your parrotfish eat one of your shrimp and the coral it guards, thus earning yourself VP.  Ah, but the relative strengths of the coral types also determines their values in VP at the end.  Very good stuff, but not something you can casually whip out, teach to someone new, and expect them to offer you any competition.

If the rating simply reflected its brilliance, this game would be rather close to a [b][bgcolor=#00cc00]&nbsp;10&nbsp;[/bgcolor][/b].

RATING: 6

What causes this apprehension to pull it out again, I don't know.  If I were to ask myself if I've had fun playing it in the past, I wouldn't hesitate to say "yes" (and I really dig the theme), but I'd be a liar if I didn't say something about it bugs me.  Until I figure out what it is, I'm forced to rate this a 6 by BGG standards.
